This notebook provides the straightforward codes to perform the SVD of our MAM cells data. 

The data contains 1k genes as rows, and 1k cells from various parts of the body and organs. Each cell has been labelled MAM or non-MAM based on the activity of a particular coexpressed gene. Our task is to use SVD and check if we get the cells that are involved in this gene coexpression. 

## Importing and preprocessing the data

### Import the data


```julia
# libraries to import csv data
using CSV, DataFrames

# import mamsubset1kCell1kGene.csv
data = CSV.read("/Users/gauthaneshwar/Documents/VSCode/EnricoColab/mamSubset1kCell1kGene.csv", DataFrame)

```

### Sample out some genes and cell labels from the data


```julia
# select and print the names of the first 5 columns
println("Random five cell labels in our data: ")
display(names(data)[rand(1:1000, 5)])

# select and print the first 5 rows
println("Random five gene labels in our data: ")
display(data[rand(1:1000, 5),1])
```

    Random five cell labels in our data:

We see that the cells have a label of the organ, if it is MAM positive, a cell number (possibly associated with the cell type), and the gene sequence at the end. The gene labels are just a text with numbers (not much to understand here). 

### Collect the column indices of the cells that are tagged as MAM


```julia
# save the labels of genes
genes = data[:,1]

# remove the first columns
data = data[:, 2:end]

# store the array of column indices whose name contains _MAM_ in it 
mamIndices = findall(x -> contains(x, "_MAM_"), names(data))

# print the indices
println(mamIndices)
```

## SVD of the data and the results

```julia
# perform an SVD of the data
using LinearAlgebra

println("Performing the SVD of the data...")
@time U, S, V = svd(Matrix(data))

data1 = U*Diagonal(S)*V'

# Check if data1 is approximately equal to data with a tolerance of 10^-4
println("Checking if the SVD is accurate upto the error of 1e-4: ", isapprox(data1, Matrix(data), atol=1e-4))
```

    Performing the SVD of the data...
      0.871627 seconds (340.89 k allocations: 76.424 MiB, 3.13% gc time, 37.41% compilation time)
    Checking if the SVD is accurate upto the error of 1e-4: true

</svg>


The above plot suggests that there are a handful of strong correlations of a bunch of genes with a bunch of cells, another small fraction of them that are very weakly correlated (with singular values < 1), and a big chunk of singular values in between that signify some decent correlation. We hope that the signatures of our MAM cells and their corresponding correlations are present in just the first few of the singular values and their corresponding vectors 

### The singular vectors of the cells

We plot the values of the first singular vector for the cells and the genes. The strong signals it picks from the cells must correspond to MAM cells since we expect the SVD to find the MAM correlation of genes with the corresponding cells. The MAM cells that we found from the cell labels earlier are marked as red. We expect all the red cells to have large values if SVD can do so.

```julia
# plot the first five eigenvectors in a separate plot
i = 1
eig_vec_cell = V[:, i]
eig_vec_gene = U[i,:]
p1 = plot(eig_vec_cell, legend=false, xlabel="Cell index", ylabel="Eigenvector $i values")
# plot the points at indices mamIndices
scatter!(p1, mamIndices, eig_vec_cell[mamIndices], markersize=2, color=:red)

p2 = plot(eig_vec_gene, legend=false,xlabel="Gene index", ylabel="Eigenvector values")

display(p1)
display(p2)
```

```julia
# print the genes that correspond to the high chance of our cell being a MAM cell
# Define the threshold
threshold = 0.05  # Replace this with your actual threshold

# Find the indices of the entries that are greater than the threshold
significant_indices = findall(x -> abs(x) > threshold, U[1,:])

println("The genes that imply strong presence of MAM cells: ")
display(genes[significant_indices])
```

</svg>



> [!summary] Conclusion
> 1. SVD can find the correlation of a set of genes corresponding to a set of cells being MaM
> 2. Only a very few singular values have the corresponding singular vectors to store the information about the correlation.
> 3. Just the vanilla SVD is quite fast for the given sample data. It can also work for larger datasets as long as the minimum number of rows and columns is manageable.


> [!attention] Limitations and work needed
> 1. The singular vectors hold information on several correlations. Some may correspond to MAM cells, others may mean some other harmless correlation between the cell and genes. Several such gene correlations can exist. So, we need to be able to distinguish them apart.
> 2. There are false positive and false negative errors if we look at a single vector. There must be a way to determine which ones correspond to MAM correlations and separate them from the other correlations. 
> 3. We need to add the MAM correlations after identifying them and add them across different singular vectors to finally conclude the genes whose expression implies MAM. 


> [!cite] [This paper](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC7771883/) says
> Compared to matrices, tensors provide greater flexibility to describe data but entail a higher computational cost. Indeed, extending familiar matrix concepts such as SVD to tensors is not straightforward ([De Silva and Lim 2008](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC7771883/#R10);Â [Kolda and Bader 2009](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC7771883/#R19);Â [Wang et al. 2017](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC7771883/#R40)), and the associated computational complexity has proven to be NP-hard ([Hillar and Lim 2013](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC7771883/#R15)).
